   201	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   202	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tPREFACE.
   203	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   204	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   205	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tThe present work is a modest effort to reproduce approximately, in modern
   206	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tmeasures, the venerable epic, Beowulf. _Approximately_, I repeat; for a
   207	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tvery close reproduction of Anglo-Saxon verse would, to a large extent, be
   208	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tprose to a modern ear.
   209	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   210	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tThe Heyne-Socin text and glossary have been closely followed. Occasionally
   211	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\ta deviation has been made, but always for what seemed good and sufficient
   212	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\treason. The translator does not aim to be an editor. Once in a while,
   213	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\thowever, he has added a conjecture of his own to the emendations quoted
   214	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tfrom the criticisms of other students of the poem.
   215	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   216	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tThis work is addressed to two classes of readers. From both of these alike
   217	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tthe translator begs sympathy and co-operation. The Anglo-Saxon scholar he
   218	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\thopes to please by adhering faithfully to the original. The student of
   219	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tEnglish literature he aims to interest by giving him, in modern garb, the
   220	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tmost ancient epic of our race. This is a bold and venturesome undertaking;
   221	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tand yet there must be some students of the Teutonic past willing to follow
   222	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\teven a daring guide, if they may read in modern phrases of the sorrows of
   223	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHrothgar, of the prowess of Beowulf, and of the feelings that stirred the
   224	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\thearts of our forefathers in their primeval homes.
   225	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   226	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tIn order to please the larger class of readers, a regular cadence has been
   227	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tused, a measure which, while retaining the essential characteristics of
   228	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tthe original, permits the reader to see ahead of him in reading.
   229	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   230	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tPerhaps every Anglo-Saxon scholar has his own theory as to how Beowulf
   231	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tshould be translated. Some have given us prose versions of what we believe
   232	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tto be a great poem. Is it any reflection on our honored Kemble and Arnold
   233	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tto say that their translations fail to show a layman that Beowulf is
   234	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tjustly called our first _epic_? Of those translators who have used verse,
   235	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tseveral have written from what would seem a mistaken point of view. Is it
   236	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tproper, for instance, that the grave and solemn speeches of Beowulf and
   237	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHrothgar be put in ballad measures, tripping lightly and airily along? Or,
   238	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tagain, is it fitting that the rough martial music of Anglo-Saxon verse be
   239	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tinterpreted to us in the smooth measures of modern blank verse? Do we hear
   240	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\twhat has been beautifully called "the clanging tread of a warrior in
   241	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tmail"?
   242	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   243	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[viii]
   244	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   245	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tOf all English translations of Beowulf, that of Professor Garnett alone
   246	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tgives any adequate idea of the chief characteristics of this great
   247	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tTeutonic epic.
   248	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   249	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tThe measure used in the present translation is believed to be as near a
   250	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\treproduction of the original as modern English affords. The cadences
   251	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tclosely resemble those used by Browning in some of his most striking
   252	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tpoems. The four stresses of the Anglo-Saxon verse are retained, and as
   253	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tmuch thesis and anacrusis is allowed as is consistent with a regular
   254	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tcadence. Alliteration has been used to a large extent; but it was thought
   255	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tthat modern ears would hardly tolerate it on every line. End-rhyme has
   256	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tbeen used occasionally; internal rhyme, sporadically. Both have some
   257	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\twarrant in Anglo-Saxon poetry. (For end-rhyme, see 1_53, 1_54; for
   258	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tinternal rhyme, 2_21, 6_40.)
   259	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   260	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tWhat Gummere[1] calls the "rime-giver" has been studiously kept; _viz._,
   261	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tthe first accented syllable in the second half-verse always carries the
   262	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\talliteration; and the last accented syllable alliterates only
   263	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tsporadically. Alternate alliteration is occasionally used as in the
   264	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\toriginal. (See 7_61, 8_5.)
   265	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   266	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tNo two accented syllables have been brought together, except occasionally
   267	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tafter a cæsural pause. (See 2_19 and 12_1.) Or, scientifically speaking,
   268	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tSievers's C type has been avoided as not consonant with the plan of
   269	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\ttranslation. Several of his types, however, constantly occur; _e.g._ A and
   270	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\ta variant (/ x | / x) (/ x x | / x); B and a variant (x / | x / ) (x x / |
   271	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tx / ); a variant of D (/ x | / x x); E (/ x x | / ). Anacrusis gives
   272	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tfurther variety to the types used in the translation.
   273	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   274	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tThe parallelisms of the original have been faithfully preserved. (_E.g._,
   275	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t1_16 and 1_17: "Lord" and "Wielder of Glory"; 1_30, 1_31, 1_32; 2_12 and
   276	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t2_13; 2_27 and 2_28; 3_5 and 3_6.) Occasionally, some loss has been
   277	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tsustained; but, on the other hand, a gain has here and there been made.
   278	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   279	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tThe effort has been made to give a decided flavor of archaism to the
   280	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\ttranslation. All words not in keeping with the spirit of the poem have
   281	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tbeen avoided. Again, though many archaic words have been used, there are
   282	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tnone, it is believed, which are not found in standard modern poetry.
   283	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   284	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[ix]
   285	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   286	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tWith these preliminary remarks, it will not be amiss to give an outline of
   287	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tthe story of the poem.
   288	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   289	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   290	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t_THE STORY._
   291	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   292	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t_Hrothgar, king of the Danes, or Scyldings, builds a great mead-hall, or
   293	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tpalace, in which he hopes to feast his liegemen and to give them presents.
   294	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tThe joy of king and retainers is, however, of short duration. Grendel, the
   295	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tmonster, is seized with hateful jealousy. He cannot brook the sounds of
   296	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tjoyance that reach him down in his fen-dwelling near the hall. Oft and
   297	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tanon he goes to the joyous building, bent on direful mischief. Thane after
   298	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tthane is ruthlessly carried off and devoured, while no one is found strong
   299	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tenough and bold enough to cope with the monster. For twelve years he
   300	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tpersecutes Hrothgar and his vassals._
   301	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   302	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t_Over sea, a day's voyage off, Beowulf, of the Geats, nephew of Higelac,
   303	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tking of the Geats, hears of Grendel's doings and of Hrothgar's misery. He
   304	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tresolves to crush the fell monster and relieve the aged king. With
   305	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tfourteen chosen companions, he sets sail for Dane-land. Reaching that
   306	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tcountry, he soon persuades Hrothgar of his ability to help him. The hours
   307	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tthat elapse before night are spent in beer-drinking and conversation. When
   308	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHrothgar's bedtime comes he leaves the hall in charge of Beowulf, telling
   309	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\thim that never before has he given to another the absolute wardship of his
   310	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tpalace. All retire to rest, Beowulf, as it were, sleeping upon his arms._
   311	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   312	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t_Grendel comes, the great march-stepper, bearing God's anger. He seizes
   313	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tand kills one of the sleeping warriors. Then he advances towards Beowulf.
   314	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tA fierce and desperate hand-to-hand struggle ensues. No arms are used,
   315	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tboth combatants trusting to strength and hand-grip. Beowulf tears
   316	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tGrendel's shoulder from its socket, and the monster retreats to his den,
   317	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\thowling and yelling with agony and fury. The wound is fatal._
   318	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   319	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t_The next morning, at early dawn, warriors in numbers flock to the hall
   320	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHeorot, to hear the news. Joy is boundless. Glee runs high. Hrothgar and
   321	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\this retainers are lavish of gratitude and of gifts._
   322	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   323	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t_Grendel's mother, however, comes the next night to avenge his death. She
   324	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tis furious and raging. While Beowulf is sleeping in a room somewhat apart
   325	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[x] from the quarters of the other warriors, she seizes one of Hrothgar's
   326	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tfavorite counsellors, and carries him off and devours him. Beowulf is
   327	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tcalled. Determined to leave Heorot entirely purified, he arms himself, and
   328	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tgoes down to look for the female monster. After traveling through the
   329	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\twaters many hours, he meets her near the sea-bottom. She drags him to her
   330	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tden. There he sees Grendel lying dead. After a desperate and almost fatal
   331	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tstruggle with the woman, he slays her, and swims upward in triumph, taking
   332	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\twith him Grendel's head._
   333	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   334	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t_Joy is renewed at Heorot. Congratulations crowd upon the victor. Hrothgar
   335	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tliterally pours treasures into the lap of Beowulf; and it is agreed among
   336	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tthe vassals of the king that Beowulf will be their next liegelord._
   337	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   338	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t_Beowulf leaves Dane-land. Hrothgar weeps and laments at his departure._
   339	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   340	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t_When the hero arrives in his own land, Higelac treats him as a
   341	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tdistinguished guest. He is the hero of the hour._
   342	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   343	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t_Beowulf subsequently becomes king of his own people, the Geats. After he
   344	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\thas been ruling for fifty years, his own neighborhood is wofully harried
   345	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tby a fire-spewing dragon. Beowulf determines to kill him. In the ensuing
   346	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tstruggle both Beowulf and the dragon are slain. The grief of the Geats is
   347	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tinexpressible. They determine, however, to leave nothing undone to honor
   348	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tthe memory of their lord. A great funeral-pyre is built, and his body is
   349	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tburnt. Then a memorial-barrow is made, visible from a great distance, that
   350	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tsailors afar may be constantly reminded of the prowess of the national
   351	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\thero of Geatland._
   352	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   353	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t_The poem closes with a glowing tribute to his bravery, his gentleness,
   354	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\this goodness of heart, and his generosity._
   355	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   356	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       *       *       *       *       *
   357	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   358	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tIt is the devout desire of this translator to hasten the day when the
   359	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tstory of Beowulf shall be as familiar to English-speaking peoples as that
   360	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tof the Iliad. Beowulf is our first great epic. It is an epitomized history
   361	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tof the life of the Teutonic races. It brings vividly before us our
   362	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tforefathers of pre-Alfredian eras, in their love of war, of sea, and of
   363	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tadventure.
   364	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   365	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tMy special thanks are due to Professors Francis A. March and James A.
   366	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHarrison, for advice, sympathy, and assistance.
   367	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t

   380	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tKluge. M.= Müllenhoff. R. = Rieger. S. = Sievers. Sw. = Sweet. t.B. = ten
   381	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tBrink. Th. = Thorpe. W. = Wülcker.

   458	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tGLOSSARY OF PROPER NAMES.
   459	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   460	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       *       *       *       *       *
   461	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   462	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[The figures refer to the divisions of the poem in which the respective
   463	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tnames occur. The large figures refer to fitts, the small, to lines in the
   464	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tfitts.]
   465	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   466	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       *       *       *       *       *
   467	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   468	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Ælfhere~.--A kinsman of Wiglaf.--36_3.
   469	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   470	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Æschere~.--Confidential friend of King Hrothgar. Elder brother of
   471	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tYrmenlaf. Killed by Grendel.--21_3; 30_89.
   472	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   473	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Beanstan~.--Father of Breca.--9_26.
   474	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   475	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Beowulf~.--Son of Scyld, the founder of the dynasty of Scyldings. Father
   476	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tof Healfdene, and grandfather of Hrothgar.--1_18; 2_1.
   477	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   478	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Beowulf~.--The hero of the poem. Sprung from the stock of Geats, son of
   479	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tEcgtheow. Brought up by his maternal grandfather Hrethel, and figuring in
   480	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tmanhood as a devoted liegeman of his uncle Higelac. A hero from his youth.
   481	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHas the strength of thirty men. Engages in a swimming-match with Breca.
   482	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tGoes to the help of Hrothgar against the monster Grendel. Vanquishes
   483	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tGrendel and his mother. Afterwards becomes king of the Geats. Late in life
   484	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tattempts to kill a fire-spewing dragon, and is slain. Is buried with great
   485	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\thonors. His memorial mound.--6_26; 7_2; 7_9; 9_3; 9_8; 12_28; 12_43; 23_1,
   486	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tetc.
   487	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   488	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Breca~.--Beowulf's opponent in the famous swimming-match.--9_8; 9_19;
   489	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t9_21; 9_22.
   490	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   491	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Brondings~.--A people ruled by Breca.--9_23.
   492	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   493	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Brosinga mene~.--A famous collar once owned by the Brosings.--19_7.
   494	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   495	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Cain~.--Progenitor of Grendel and other monsters.--2_56; 20_11.
   496	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   497	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Dæghrefn~.--A warrior of the Hugs, killed by Beowulf.--35_40.
   498	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   499	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Danes~.--Subjects of Scyld and his descendants, and hence often called
   500	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tScyldings. Other names for them are Victory-Scyldings, Honor-Scyldings,
   501	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tArmor-Danes, Bright-Danes, East-Danes, West-Danes, North-Danes,
   502	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tSouth-Danes, Ingwins, Hrethmen.--1_1; 2_1; 3_2; 5_14; 7_1, etc.
   503	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   504	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Ecglaf~.--Father of Unferth, who taunts Beowulf.--9_1.
   505	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   506	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Ecgtheow~.--Father of Beowulf, the hero of the poem. A widely-known
   507	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tWægmunding warrior. Marries Hrethel's daughter. After slaying Heatholaf, a
   508	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tWylfing, he flees his country.--7_3; 5_6; 8_4.
   509	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   510	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Ecgwela~.--A king of the Danes before Scyld.--25_60.
   511	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   512	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[xiv]
   513	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   514	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Elan~.--Sister of Hrothgar, and probably wife of Ongentheow, king of the
   515	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tSwedes.--2_10.
   516	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   517	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Eagle Cape~.--A promontory in Geat-land, under which took place Beowulf's
   518	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tlast encounter.--41_87.
   519	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   520	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Eadgils~.--Son of Ohthere and brother of Eanmund.--34_2.
   521	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   522	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Eanmund~.--Son of Ohthere and brother of Eadgils. The reference to these
   523	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tbrothers is vague, and variously understood. Heyne supposes as follows:
   524	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tRaising a revolt against their father, they are obliged to leave Sweden.
   525	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tThey go to the land of the Geats; with what intention, is not known, but
   526	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tprobably to conquer and plunder. The Geatish king, Heardred, is slain by
   527	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tone of the brothers, probably Eanmund.--36_10; 31_54 to 31_60; 33_66 to
   528	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t34_6.
   529	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   530	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Eofor~.--A Geatish hero who slays Ongentheow in war, and is rewarded by
   531	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHygelac with the hand of his only daughter.--41_18; 41_48.
   532	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   533	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Eormenric~.--A Gothic king, from whom Hama took away the famous Brosinga
   534	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tmene.--19_9.
   535	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   536	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Eomær~.--Son of Offa and Thrytho, king and queen of the Angles.--28_69.
   537	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   538	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Finn~.--King of the North-Frisians and the Jutes. Marries Hildeburg. At
   539	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\this court takes place the horrible slaughter in which the Danish general,
   540	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHnæf, fell. Later on, Finn himself is slain by Danish warriors.--17_18;
   541	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t17_30; 17_44; 18_4; 18_23.
   542	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   543	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Fin-land~.--The country to which Beowulf was driven by the currents in
   544	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\this swimming-match.--10_22.
   545	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   546	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Fitela~.--Son and nephew of King Sigemund, whose praises are sung in
   547	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tXIV.--14_42; 14_53.
   548	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   549	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Folcwalda~.--Father of Finn.--17_38.
   550	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   551	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Franks~.--Introduced occasionally in referring to the death of
   552	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHigelac.--19_19; 40_21; 40_24.
   553	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   554	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Frisians~.--A part of them are ruled by Finn. Some of them were engaged
   555	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tin the struggle in which Higelac was slain.--17_20; 17_42; 17_52; 40_21.
   556	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   557	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Freaware~.--Daughter of King Hrothgar. Married to Ingeld, a Heathobard
   558	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tprince.--29_60; 30_32.
   559	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   560	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Froda~.--King of the Heathobards, and father of Ingeld.--29_62.
   561	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   562	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Garmund~.--Father of Offa.--28_71.
   563	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   564	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Geats, Geatmen~.--The race to which the hero of the poem belongs. Also
   565	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tcalled Weder-Geats, or Weders, War-Geats, Sea-Geats. They are ruled by
   566	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHrethel, Hæthcyn, Higelac, and Beowulf.--4_7; 7_4; 10_45; 11_8; 27_14;
   567	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t28_8.
   568	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   569	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Gepids~.--Named in connection with the Danes and Swedes.--35_34.
   570	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   571	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Grendel~.--A monster of the race of Cain. Dwells in the fens and moors.
   572	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tIs furiously envious when he hears sounds of joy in Hrothgar's palace.
   573	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tCauses the king untold agony for years. Is finally conquered by Beowulf,
   574	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tand dies of his wound. His hand and arm are hung up in Hrothgar's hall
   575	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHeorot. His head is cut off by Beowulf when he goes down to fight with
   576	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tGrendel's mother.--2_50; 3_1; 3_13; 8_19; 11_17; 12_2; 13_27; 15_3.
   577	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   578	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Guthlaf~.--A Dane of Hnæf's party.--18_24.
   579	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   580	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Half-Danes~.--Branch of the Danes to which Hnæf belonged.--17_19.
   581	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   582	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[xv]
   583	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   584	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Halga~.--Surnamed the Good. Younger brother of Hrothgar.--2_9.
   585	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   586	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hama~.--Takes the Brosinga mene from Eormenric.--19_7.
   587	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   588	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hæreth~.--Father of Higelac's queen, Hygd.--28_39; 29_18.
   589	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   590	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hæthcyn~.--Son of Hrethel and brother of Higelac. Kills his brother
   591	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHerebeald accidentally. Is slain at Ravenswood, fighting against
   592	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tOngentheow.--34_43; 35_23; 40_32.
   593	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   594	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Helmings~.--The race to which Queen Wealhtheow belonged.--10_63.
   595	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   596	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Heming~.--A kinsman of Garmund, perhaps nephew.--28_54; 28_70.
   597	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t

   612	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Healfdene~.--Grandson of Scyld and father of Hrothgar. Ruled the Danes
   613	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tlong and well.--2_5; 4_1; 8_14.
   614	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   615	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Heardred~.--Son of Higelac and Hygd, king and queen of the Geats.
   616	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tSucceeds his father, with Beowulf as regent. Is slain by the sons of
   617	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tOhthere.--31_56; 33_63; 33_75.
   618	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   619	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Heathobards~.--Race of Lombards, of which Froda is king. After Froda

   621	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tdaughter, Freaware, in order to heal the feud.--30_1; 30_6.

   633	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Heort~, ~Heorot~.--The great mead-hall which King Hrothgar builds. It is
   634	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tinvaded by Grendel for twelve years. Finally cleansed by Beowulf, the
   635	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tGeat. It is called Heort on account of the hart-antlers which decorate
   636	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tit.--2_25; 3_32; 3_52.
   637	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   638	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hildeburg~.--Wife of Finn, daughter of Hoce, and related to

   649	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hrethel~.--King of the Geats, father of Higelac, and grandfather of

   662	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   663	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hrothgar~.--The Danish king who built the hall Heort, but was long unable
   664	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tto enjoy it on account of Grendel's persecutions. Marries Wealhtheow, a
   665	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHelming lady. Has two sons and a daughter. Is a typical Teutonic king,
   666	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tlavish of gifts. A devoted liegelord, as his lamentations over slain
   667	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tliegemen prove. Also very appreciative of kindness, as is shown by his
   668	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tloving gratitude to Beowulf.--2_9; 2_12; 4_1; 8_10; 15_1; etc., etc.
   669	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t

   672	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hrothulf~.--Probably a son of Halga, younger brother of Hrothgar.

   674	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t

   679	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   680	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hun~.--A Frisian warrior, probably general of the Hetwars. Gives Hengest

   682	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   683	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hunferth~.--Sometimes used for Unferth.
   684	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   685	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Hygelac~, ~Higelac~.--King of the Geats, uncle and liegelord of Beowulf,
   686	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tthe hero of the poem.--His second wife is the lovely Hygd, daughter of
   687	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHæreth. The son of their union is Heardred. Is slain in a war with the
   688	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHugs, Franks, and Frisians combined. Beowulf is regent, and afterwards
   689	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tking of the Geats.--4_6; 5_4; 28_34; 29_9; 29_21; 31_56.
   690	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t

   692	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tindications that she married Beowulf after she became a widow.--28_37.
   693	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   694	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Ingeld~.--Son of the Heathobard king, Froda. Marries Hrothgar's daughter,

   703	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Merewing~.--A Frankish king, probably engaged in the war in which Higelac

   708	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Offa~.--King of the Angles, and son of Garmund. Marries the terrible

   710	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   711	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Ohthere~.--Son of Ongentheow, king of the Swedes. He is father of Eanmund

   716	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Ongentheow~.--King of Sweden, of the Scylfing dynasty. Married, perhaps,

   726	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   727	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Scyld~.--Founder of the dynasty to which Hrothgar, his father, and
   728	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tgrandfather belonged. He dies, and his body is put on a vessel, and set

   730	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t1_19; 1_27.

   735	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t

   746	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Thrytho~.--Wife of Offa, king of the Angles. Known for her fierce and

   764	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t

   768	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Wealhtheow~.--Wife of Hrothgar. Her queenly courtesy is well shown in the

   774	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tbuilt.--38_52; 42_76.
   775	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   776	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Wiglaf~.--Son of Wihstan, and related to Beowulf. He remains faithful to
   777	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tBeowulf in the fatal struggle with the fire-drake. Would rather die than
   778	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tleave his lord in his dire emergency.--36_1; 36_3; 36_28.
   779	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   780	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Wonred~.--Father of Wulf and Eofor.--41_20; 41_26.
   781	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   782	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t~Wulf~.--Son of Wonred. Engaged in the battle between Higelac's and
   783	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tOngentheow's forces, and had a hand-to-hand fight with Ongentheow himself.
   784	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tOngentheow disables him, and is thereupon slain by Eofor.--41_19; 41_29.
   785	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t

   899	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t

   904	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tUNNETHE.--Difficult.
   905	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t

   919	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tGlory,' 'Wielder of Worship.'

   939	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tI.
   940	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   941	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tTHE LIFE AND DEATH OF SCYLD.
   942	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   943	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   944	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{The famous race of Spear-Danes.}
   945	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   946	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Lo! the Spear-Danes' glory through splendid achievements
   947	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The folk-kings' former fame we have heard of,
   948	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          How princes displayed then their prowess-in-battle.
   949	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   950	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Scyld, their mighty king, in honor of whom they are often called
   951	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tScyldings. He is the great-grandfather of Hrothgar, so prominent in the
   952	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tpoem.}
   953	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   954	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Oft Scyld the Scefing from scathers in numbers
   955	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t        5 From many a people their mead-benches tore.
   956	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Since first he found him friendless and wretched,
   957	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The earl had had terror: comfort he got for it,
   958	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Waxed 'neath the welkin, world-honor gained,
   959	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Till all his neighbors o'er sea were compelled to
   960	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       10 Bow to his bidding and bring him their tribute:
   961	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          An excellent atheling! After was borne him
   962	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   963	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{A son is born to him, who receives the name of Beowulf--a name afterwards
   964	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tmade so famous by the hero of the poem.}
   965	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   966	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          A son and heir, young in his dwelling,
   967	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Whom God-Father sent to solace the people.
   968	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          He had marked the misery malice had caused them,
   969	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       15 [1]That reaved of their rulers they wretched had erstwhile[2]
   970	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Long been afflicted. The Lord, in requital,
   971	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Wielder of Glory, with world-honor blessed him.
   972	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Famed was Beowulf, far spread the glory
   973	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Of Scyld's great son in the lands of the Danemen.
   974	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   975	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[2]
   976	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   977	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{The ideal Teutonic king lavishes gifts on his vassals.}
   978	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   979	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       20 So the carle that is young, by kindnesses rendered
   980	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The friends of his father, with fees in abundance
   981	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Must be able to earn that when age approacheth
   982	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Eager companions aid him requitingly,
   983	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          When war assaults him serve him as liegemen:
   984	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       25 By praise-worthy actions must honor be got
   985	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          'Mong all of the races. At the hour that was fated
   986	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   987	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Scyld dies at the hour appointed by Fate.}
   988	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   989	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Scyld then departed to the All-Father's keeping
   990	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Warlike to wend him; away then they bare him
   991	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          To the flood of the current, his fond-loving comrades,
   992	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       30 As himself he had bidden, while the friend of the Scyldings
   993	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Word-sway wielded, and the well-lovèd land-prince
   994	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Long did rule them.[3] The ring-stemmèd vessel,
   995	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Bark of the atheling, lay there at anchor,
   996	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Icy in glimmer and eager for sailing;
   997	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
   998	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{By his own request, his body is laid on a vessel and wafted seaward.}
   999	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1000	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       35 The belovèd leader laid they down there,
  1001	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Giver of rings, on the breast of the vessel,
  1002	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The famed by the mainmast. A many of jewels,
  1003	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Of fretted embossings, from far-lands brought over,
  1004	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Was placed near at hand then; and heard I not ever
  1005	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       40 That a folk ever furnished a float more superbly
  1006	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          With weapons of warfare, weeds for the battle,
  1007	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Bills and burnies; on his bosom sparkled
  1008	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Many a jewel that with him must travel
  1009	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          On the flush of the flood afar on the current.
  1010	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       45 And favors no fewer they furnished him soothly,
  1011	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Excellent folk-gems, than others had given him
  1012	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1013	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{He leaves Daneland on the breast of a bark.}
  1014	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1015	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Who when first he was born outward did send him
  1016	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Lone on the main, the merest of infants:
  1017	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          And a gold-fashioned standard they stretched under heaven
  1018	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[3]    50 High o'er his head, let the holm-currents bear him,
  1019	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Seaward consigned him: sad was their spirit,
  1020	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Their mood very mournful. Men are not able
  1021	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1022	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{No one knows whither the boat drifted.}
  1023	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1024	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Soothly to tell us, they in halls who reside,[4]
  1025	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Heroes under heaven, to what haven he hied.
  1026	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1027	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    [1] For the 'Þæt' of verse 15, Sievers suggests 'Þá' (= which). If
  1028	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    this be accepted, the sentence 'He had ... afflicted' will read: _He_
  1029	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    (_i.e._ God) _had perceived the malice-caused sorrow which they,
  1030	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    lordless, had formerly long endured_.
  1031	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1032	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    [2] For 'aldor-léase' (15) Gr. suggested 'aldor-ceare': _He perceived
  1033	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    their distress, that they formerly had suffered life-sorrow a long
  1034	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    while_.
  1035	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1036	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    [3] A very difficult passage. 'Áhte' (31) has no object. H. supplies
  1037	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    'geweald' from the context; and our translation is based upon this
  1038	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    assumption, though it is far from satisfactory. Kl. suggests
  1039	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    'lændagas' for 'lange': _And the beloved land-prince enjoyed (had) his
  1040	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    transitory days (i.e. lived)_. B. suggests a dislocation; but this is
  1041	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    a dangerous doctrine, pushed rather far by that eminent scholar.
  1042	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1043	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    [4] The reading of the H.-So. text has been quite closely followed;
  1044	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    but some eminent scholars read 'séle-rædenne' for 'sele-rædende.' If
  1045	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    that be adopted, the passage will read: _Men cannot tell us, indeed,
  1046	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    the order of Fate, etc._ 'Sele-rædende' has two things to support it:
  1047	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    (1) v. 1347; (2) it affords a parallel to 'men' in v. 50.
  1048	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t

  1052	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tII.
  1053	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1054	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tSCYLD'S SUCCESSORS.--HROTHGAR'S GREAT MEAD-HALL.
  1055	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1056	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1057	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Beowulf succeeds his father Scyld}
  1058	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1059	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          In the boroughs then Beowulf, bairn of the Scyldings,
  1060	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Belovèd land-prince, for long-lasting season
  1061	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Was famed mid the folk (his father departed,
  1062	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The prince from his dwelling), till afterward sprang
  1063	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t        5 Great-minded Healfdene; the Danes in his lifetime
  1064	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          He graciously governed, grim-mooded, agèd.
  1065	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1066	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Healfdene's birth.}
  1067	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1068	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Four bairns of his body born in succession
  1069	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Woke in the world, war-troopers' leader
  1070	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Heorogar, Hrothgar, and Halga the good;
  1071	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       10 Heard I that Elan was Ongentheow's consort,
  1072	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1073	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{He has three sons--one of them, Hrothgar--and a daughter named Elan.
  1074	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tHrothgar becomes a mighty king.}
  1075	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1076	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The well-beloved bedmate of the War-Scylfing leader.
  1077	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Then glory in battle to Hrothgar was given,
  1078	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Waxing of war-fame, that willingly kinsmen
  1079	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Obeyed his bidding, till the boys grew to manhood,
  1080	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       15 A numerous band.  It burned in his spirit
  1081	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          To urge his folk to found a great building,
  1082	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          A mead-hall grander than men of the era
  1083	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1084	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{He is eager to build a great hall in which he may feast his retainers}
  1085	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1086	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Ever had heard of, and in it to share
  1087	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          With young and old all of the blessings
  1088	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       20 The Lord had allowed him, save life and retainers.
  1089	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Then the work I find afar was assigned
  1090	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[4]       To many races in middle-earth's regions,
  1091	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          To adorn the great folk-hall. In due time it happened
  1092	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Early 'mong men, that 'twas finished entirely,
  1093	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       25 The greatest of hall-buildings; Heorot he named it
  1094	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1095	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{The hall is completed, and is called Heort, or Heorot.}
  1096	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1097	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Who wide-reaching word-sway wielded 'mong earlmen.
  1098	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          His promise he brake not, rings he lavished,
  1099	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Treasure at banquet. Towered the hall up
  1100	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          High and horn-crested, huge between antlers:
  1101	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       30 It battle-waves bided, the blasting fire-demon;
  1102	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Ere long then from hottest hatred must sword-wrath
  1103	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Arise for a woman's husband and father.
  1104	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Then the mighty war-spirit[1] endured for a season,
  1105	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1106	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{The Monster Grendel is madly envious of the Danemen's joy.}
  1107	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1108	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Bore it bitterly, he who bided in darkness,
  1109	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       35 That light-hearted laughter loud in the building
  1110	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Greeted him daily; there was dulcet harp-music,
  1111	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Clear song of the singer. He said that was able
  1112	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1113	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{[The course of the story is interrupted by a short reference to some old
  1114	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\taccount of the creation.]}
  1115	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1116	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          To tell from of old earthmen's beginnings,
  1117	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          That Father Almighty earth had created,
  1118	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       40 The winsome wold that the water encircleth,
  1119	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Set exultingly the sun's and the moon's beams
  1120	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          To lavish their lustre on land-folk and races,
  1121	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          And earth He embellished in all her regions
  1122	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          With limbs and leaves; life He bestowed too
  1123	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       45 On all the kindreds that live under heaven.
  1124	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1125	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{The glee of the warriors is overcast by a horrible dread.}
  1126	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1127	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          So blessed with abundance, brimming with joyance,
  1128	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The warriors abided, till a certain one gan to
  1129	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Dog them with deeds of direfullest malice,
  1130	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          A foe in the hall-building: this horrible stranger[2]
  1131	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       50 Was Grendel entitled, the march-stepper famous
  1132	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Who[3] dwelt in the moor-fens, the marsh and the fastness;
  1133	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The wan-mooded being abode for a season
  1134	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[5]       In the land of the giants, when the Lord and Creator
  1135	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Had banned him and branded. For that bitter murder,
  1136	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       55 The killing of Abel, all-ruling Father
  1137	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1138	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Cain is referred to as a progenitor of Grendel, and of monsters in
  1139	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tgeneral.}
  1140	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1141	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The kindred of Cain crushed with His vengeance;
  1142	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          In the feud He rejoiced not, but far away drove him
  1143	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          From kindred and kind, that crime to atone for,
  1144	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Meter of Justice. Thence ill-favored creatures,
  1145	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       60 Elves and giants, monsters of ocean,
  1146	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Came into being, and the giants that longtime
  1147	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Grappled with God; He gave them requital.
  1148	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1149	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    [1] R. and t. B. prefer 'ellor-gæst' to 'ellen-gæst' (86): _Then the
  1150	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    stranger from afar endured, etc._
  1151	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1152	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    [2] Some authorities would translate '_demon_' instead of

  1154	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1155	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    [3] Some authorities arrange differently, and render: _Who dwelt in
  1156	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    the moor-fens, the marsh and the fastness, the land of the

  1162	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tIII.
  1163	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1164	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tGRENDEL THE MURDERER.
  1165	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1166	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1167	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Grendel attacks the sleeping heroes}
  1168	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1169	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          When the sun was sunken, he set out to visit
  1170	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The lofty hall-building, how the Ring-Danes had used it
  1171	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          For beds and benches when the banquet was over.
  1172	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Then he found there reposing many a noble
  1173	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t        5 Asleep after supper; sorrow the heroes,[1]
  1174	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Misery knew not. The monster of evil
  1175	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Greedy and cruel tarried but little,
  1176	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1177	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{He drags off thirty of them, and devours them}
  1178	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1179	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Fell and frantic, and forced from their slumbers
  1180	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Thirty of thanemen; thence he departed
  1181	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       10 Leaping and laughing, his lair to return to,
  1182	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          With surfeit of slaughter sallying homeward.
  1183	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          In the dusk of the dawning, as the day was just breaking,
  1184	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Was Grendel's prowess revealed to the warriors:
  1185	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1186	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{A cry of agony goes up, when Grendel's horrible deed is fully realized.}
  1187	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1188	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Then, his meal-taking finished, a moan was uplifted,
  1189	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       15 Morning-cry mighty. The man-ruler famous,
  1190	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The long-worthy atheling, sat very woful,
  1191	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Suffered great sorrow, sighed for his liegemen,
  1192	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[6]       When they had seen the track of the hateful pursuer,
  1193	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The spirit accursèd: too crushing that sorrow,
  1194	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1195	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{The monster returns the next night.}
  1196	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1197	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       20 Too loathsome and lasting. Not longer he tarried,
  1198	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          But one night after continued his slaughter
  1199	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Shameless and shocking, shrinking but little
  1200	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          From malice and murder; they mastered him fully.
  1201	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          He was easy to find then who otherwhere looked for
  1202	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       25 A pleasanter place of repose in the lodges,
  1203	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          A bed in the bowers. Then was brought to his notice
  1204	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Told him truly by token apparent
  1205	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The hall-thane's hatred: he held himself after
  1206	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Further and faster who the foeman did baffle.
  1207	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       30 [2]So ruled he and strongly strove against justice
  1208	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Lone against all men, till empty uptowered
  1209	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t

  1211	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1212	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The choicest of houses. Long was the season:
  1213	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Twelve-winters' time torture suffered
  1214	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The friend of the Scyldings, every affliction,
  1215	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       35 Endless agony; hence it after[3] became
  1216	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Certainly known to the children of men
  1217	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Sadly in measures, that long against Hrothgar
  1218	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Grendel struggled:--his grudges he cherished,
  1219	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Murderous malice, many a winter,
  1220	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       40 Strife unremitting, and peacefully wished he
  1221	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          [4]Life-woe to lift from no liegeman at all of
  1222	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The men of the Dane-folk, for money to settle,
  1223	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          No counsellor needed count for a moment
  1224	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[7]       On handsome amends at the hands of the murderer;
  1225	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1226	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Grendel is unremitting in his persecutions.}
  1227	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1228	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       45 The monster of evil fiercely did harass,
  1229	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The ill-planning death-shade, both elder and younger,
  1230	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Trapping and tricking them. He trod every night then
  1231	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The mist-covered moor-fens; men do not know where
  1232	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Witches and wizards wander and ramble.
  1233	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       50 So the foe of mankind many of evils
  1234	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Grievous injuries, often accomplished,
  1235	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Horrible hermit; Heort he frequented,
  1236	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Gem-bedecked palace, when night-shades had fallen
  1237	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t

  1243	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t

  1253	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Gifts and offerings, earnestly prayed they

  1255	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Their people's oppression. Such practice they used then,
  1256	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Hope of the heathen; hell they remembered
  1257	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       65 In innermost spirit, God they knew not,
  1258	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t

  1263	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The Wielder of Glory. Woe will be his who
  1264	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Through furious hatred his spirit shall drive to
  1265	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       70 The clutch of the fire, no comfort shall look for,

  1267	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Living his life-days, his Lord may face

  1312	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          So Healfdene's kinsman constantly mused on
  1313	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          His long-lasting sorrow; the battle-thane clever
  1314	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Was not anywise able evils to 'scape from:
  1315	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Too crushing the sorrow that came to the people,
  1316	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t        5 Loathsome and lasting the life-grinding torture,
  1317	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1318	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Beowulf, the Geat, hero of the poem, hears of Hrothgar's sorrow, and
  1319	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tresolves to go to his assistance.}
  1320	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1321	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Greatest of night-woes. So Higelac's liegeman,
  1322	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Good amid Geatmen, of Grendel's achievements
  1323	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Heard in his home:[1] of heroes then living
  1324	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          He was stoutest and strongest, sturdy and noble.
  1325	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       10 He bade them prepare him a bark that was trusty;
  1326	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          He said he the war-king would seek o'er the ocean,
  1327	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The folk-leader noble, since he needed retainers.
  1328	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          For the perilous project prudent companions
  1329	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Chided him little, though loving him dearly;
  1330	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       15 They egged the brave atheling, augured him glory.
  1331	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t

  1333	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t

  1335	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Had liegemen selected, likest to prove them
  1336	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Trustworthy warriors; with fourteen companions
  1337	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The vessel he looked for; a liegeman then showed them,
  1338	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       20 A sea-crafty man, the bounds of the country.

  1340	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The craft by the cliff. Clomb to the prow then
  1341	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Well-equipped warriors: the wave-currents twisted

  1343	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       25 On the breast of the vessel bright-shining jewels,

  1345	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Warmen the wood-ship, on its wished-for adventure.

  1382	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       50 A high riding ship o'er the shoals of the waters,

  1386	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Danish dominions with army of war-ships.

  1388	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Hither to come; of kinsmen's approval,
  1389	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Word-leave of warriors, I ween that ye surely
  1390	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t

  1395	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       60 Than is one of your number, a hero in armor;
  1396	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          No low-ranking fellow[4] adorned with his weapons,

  1457	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          He lived with the people, ere he passed on his journey,
  1458	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Old from his dwelling; each of the counsellors

  1465	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[11]      Folk-troop's defender: be free in thy counsels!
  1466	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          To the noble one bear we a weighty commission,
  1467	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The helm of the Danemen; we shall hide, I ween,
  1468	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1469	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Is it true that a monster is slaying Danish heroes?}
  1470	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1471	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       15 Naught of our message. Thou know'st if it happen,
  1472	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          As we soothly heard say, that some savage despoiler,
  1473	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Some hidden pursuer, on nights that are murky
  1474	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          By deeds very direful 'mid the Danemen exhibits
  1475	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Hatred unheard of, horrid destruction
  1476	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       20 And the falling of dead. From feelings least selfish
  1477	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1478	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{I can help your king to free himself from this horrible creature.}
  1479	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1480	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          I am able to render counsel to Hrothgar,
  1481	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          How he, wise and worthy, may worst the destroyer,
  1482	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          If the anguish of sorrow should ever be lessened,[1]
  1483	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Comfort come to him, and care-waves grow cooler,
  1484	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       25 Or ever hereafter he agony suffer
  1485	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          And troublous distress, while towereth upward
  1486	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The handsomest of houses high on the summit."
  1487	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1488	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{The coast-guard reminds Beowulf that it is easier to say than to do.}
  1489	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1490	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Bestriding his stallion, the strand-watchman answered,
  1491	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The doughty retainer: "The difference surely
  1492	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       30 'Twixt words and works, the warlike shield-bearer
  1493	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Who judgeth wisely well shall determine.
  1494	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          This band, I hear, beareth no malice
  1495	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1496	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{I am satisfied of your good intentions, and shall lead you to the
  1497	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tpalace.}
  1498	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1499	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          To the prince of the Scyldings. Pass ye then onward
  1500	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          With weapons and armor. I shall lead you in person;
  1501	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       35 To my war-trusty vassals command I shall issue
  1502	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          To keep from all injury your excellent vessel,
  1503	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1504	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Your boat shall be well cared for during your stay here.}
  1505	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1506	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Your fresh-tarred craft, 'gainst every opposer
  1507	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Close by the sea-shore, till the curved-neckèd bark shall
  1508	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Waft back again the well-beloved hero
  1509	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       40 O'er the way of the water to Weder dominions.
  1510	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1511	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{He again compliments Beowulf.}
  1512	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1513	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          To warrior so great 'twill be granted sure
  1514	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          In the storm of strife to stand secure."
  1515	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Onward they fared then (the vessel lay quiet,
  1516	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The broad-bosomed bark was bound by its cable,
  1517	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[12]   45 Firmly at anchor); the boar-signs glistened[2]
  1518	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Bright on the visors vivid with gilding,
  1519	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Blaze-hardened, brilliant; the boar acted warden.
  1520	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The heroes hastened, hurried the liegemen,
  1521	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1522	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{The land is perhaps rolling.}
  1523	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1524	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Descended together, till they saw the great palace,
  1525	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       50 The well-fashioned wassail-hall wondrous and gleaming:
  1526	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1527	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Heorot flashes on their view.}
  1528	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1529	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          'Mid world-folk and kindreds that was widest reputed
  1530	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Of halls under heaven which the hero abode in;
  1531	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Its lustre enlightened lands without number.
  1532	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Then the battle-brave hero showed them the glittering
  1533	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       55 Court of the bold ones, that they easily thither
  1534	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Might fare on their journey; the aforementioned warrior
  1535	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Turning his courser, quoth as he left them:
  1536	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1537	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{The coast-guard, having discharged his duty, bids them God-speed.}
  1538	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1539	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          "'Tis time I were faring; Father Almighty
  1540	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Grant you His grace, and give you to journey
  1541	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       60 Safe on your mission! To the sea I will get me
  1542	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          'Gainst hostile warriors as warden to stand."
  1543	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1544	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t    [1] 'Edwendan' (280) B. takes to be the subs. 'edwenden' (cf. 1775);

  1557	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1558	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tBEOWULF INTRODUCES HIMSELF AT THE PALACE.
  1559	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1560	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1561	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The highway glistened with many-hued pebble,
  1562	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          A by-path led the liegemen together.
  1563	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          [1]Firm and hand-locked the war-burnie glistened,
  1564	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The ring-sword radiant rang 'mid the armor
  1565	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t        5 As the party was approaching the palace together
  1566	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1567	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{They set their arms and armor against the wall.}
  1568	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1569	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          In warlike equipments. 'Gainst the wall of the building
  1570	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Their wide-fashioned war-shields they weary did set then,
  1571	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[13]      Battle-shields sturdy; benchward they turned then;
  1572	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Their battle-sarks rattled, the gear of the heroes;
  1573	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       10 The lances stood up then, all in a cluster,
  1574	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The arms of the seamen, ashen-shafts mounted
  1575	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          With edges of iron: the armor-clad troopers
  1576	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1577	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{A Danish hero asks them whence and why they are come.}
  1578	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1579	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Were decked with weapons. Then a proud-mooded hero
  1580	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Asked of the champions questions of lineage:
  1581	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       15 "From what borders bear ye your battle-shields plated,
  1582	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Gilded and gleaming, your gray-colored burnies,
  1583	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Helmets with visors and heap of war-lances?--
  1584	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          To Hrothgar the king I am servant and liegeman.
  1585	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          'Mong folk from far-lands found I have never
  1586	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1587	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{He expresses no little admiration for the strangers.}
  1588	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1589	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       20 Men so many of mien more courageous.
  1590	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          I ween that from valor, nowise as outlaws,
  1591	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          But from greatness of soul ye sought for King Hrothgar."
  1592	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1593	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Beowulf replies.}
  1594	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1595	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Then the strength-famous earlman answer rendered,
  1596	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The proud-mooded Wederchief replied to his question,
  1597	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1598	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{We are Higelac's table-companions, and bear an important commission to
  1599	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tyour prince.}
  1600	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1601	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       25 Hardy 'neath helmet: "Higelac's mates are we;
  1602	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Beowulf hight I. To the bairn of Healfdene,
  1603	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The famous folk-leader, I freely will tell
  1604	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          To thy prince my commission, if pleasantly hearing
  1605	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          He'll grant we may greet him so gracious to all men."
  1606	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       30 Wulfgar replied then (he was prince of the Wendels,
  1607	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          His boldness of spirit was known unto many,
  1608	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          His prowess and prudence): "The prince of the Scyldings,
  1609	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1610	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Wulfgar, the thane, says that he will go and ask Hrothgar whether he will
  1611	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\tsee the strangers.}
  1612	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1613	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The friend-lord of Danemen, I will ask of thy journey,
  1614	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The giver of rings, as thou urgest me do it,
  1615	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       35 The folk-chief famous, and inform thee early
  1616	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          What answer the good one mindeth to render me."
  1617	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          He turned then hurriedly where Hrothgar was sitting,
  1618	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          [2]Old and hoary, his earlmen attending him;
  1619	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The strength-famous went till he stood at the shoulder
  1620	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       40 Of the lord of the Danemen, of courteous thanemen
  1621	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          The custom he minded. Wulfgar addressed then
  1622	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          His friendly liegelord: "Folk of the Geatmen
  1623	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1624	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t[14]
  1625	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1626	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{He thereupon urges his liegelord to receive the visitors courteously.}
  1627	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1628	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          O'er the way of the waters are wafted hither,
  1629	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Faring from far-lands: the foremost in rank
  1630	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       45 The battle-champions Beowulf title.
  1631	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          They make this petition: with thee, O my chieftain,
  1632	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          To be granted a conference; O gracious King Hrothgar,
  1633	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Friendly answer refuse not to give them!
  1634	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1635	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t{Hrothgar, too, is struck with Beowulf's appearance.}
  1636	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
  1637	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          In war-trappings weeded worthy they seem
  1638	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t       50 Of earls to be honored; sure the atheling is doughty
  1639	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t          Who headed the heroes hitherward coming."
  1640	BeowulfbyJLesslieHall.txt\t
